<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Biochemistry</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of chemical processes in living organisms</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Biological chemistry" and "Physiological chemistry" redirect here. For the journals, see <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry_(journal)" title="Biochemistry (journal)">Biochemistry (journal)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biological_Chemistry_(journal)" title="Biological Chemistry (journal)">Biological Chemistry (journal)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Biochemistry</b> or <b>biological chemistry</b> is the study of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_process" title="Chemical process">chemical processes</a> within and relating to living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>  A sub-discipline of both <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, biochemistry may be divided into three fields: <a href="/wiki/Structural_biology" title="Structural biology">structural biology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymology" title="Enzymology">enzymology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>.  Over the last decades of the 20th century, biochemistry has become successful at explaining living processes through these three disciplines.  Almost all <a href="/wiki/List_of_life_sciences" title="List of life sciences">areas of the life sciences</a> are being uncovered and developed through biochemical methodology and research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voet_2005_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_2005-2">[2]</a></sup>  Biochemistry focuses on understanding the chemical basis which allows <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biological molecules</a> to give rise to the processes that occur within living <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> and between cells,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karp2009_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karp2009-3">[3]</a></sup> in turn relating greatly to the understanding of <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a>, as well as organism structure and function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-4">[4]</a></sup> Biochemistry is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, which is the study of the <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular</a> mechanisms of biological phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn_1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn_1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of biochemistry deals with the structures, bonding, functions, and interactions of biological <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>  They provide the structure of cells and perform many of the functions associated with life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biology_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biology-7">[7]</a></sup>  The chemistry of the cell also depends upon the reactions of small <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>.  These can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inorganic" title="Inorganic">inorganic</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> ions) or <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, which are used to <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">synthesize proteins</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marks_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-8">[8]</a></sup>  The mechanisms used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_energy" title="Cell energy">cells to harness energy</a> from their environment via <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a> are known as <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>.  The findings of biochemistry are applied primarily in <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.  In medicine, <a href="/wiki/Biochemist" title="Biochemist">biochemists</a> investigate the causes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">cures</a> of <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>  Nutrition studies how to maintain health and wellness and also the effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutritional_deficiencies" title="Nutritional deficiencies">nutritional deficiencies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FFL2010_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FFL2010-10">[10]</a></sup>  In agriculture, biochemists investigate <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>. Improving crop cultivation, crop storage, and <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a> are also goals.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>

</li>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_biochemistry" title="History of biochemistry">History of biochemistry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_(1896-1957)_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3822" data-file-width="3098" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg/170px-Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg/255px-Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg/340px-Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_%281896-1957%29_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerty_Theresa_Radnitz_Cori_(1896-1957)_and_Carl_Ferdinand_Cori_-_restoration1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Cori" title="Carl Cori">Carl Cori</a> jointly won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize</a> in 1947 for their discovery of  the <a href="/wiki/Cori_cycle" title="Cori cycle">Cori cycle</a> at RPMI.</div></div></div>
<p>At its most comprehensive definition, biochemistry can be seen as a study of the components and composition of living things and how they come together to become life. In this sense, the history of biochemistry may therefore go back as far as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_of_science_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-history_of_science-11">[11]</a></sup> However, biochemistry as a specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_discipline" title="Scientific discipline">scientific discipline</a> began sometime in the 19th century, or a little earlier, depending on which aspect of biochemistry is being focused on. Some argued that the beginning of biochemistry may have been the discovery of the first <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diastase" title="Diastase">diastase</a> (now called <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a>), in 1833 by <a href="/wiki/Anselme_Payen" title="Anselme Payen">Anselme Payen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> while others considered <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Buchner" title="Eduard Buchner">Eduard Buchner</a>'s first demonstration of a complex biochemical process <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">alcoholic fermentation</a> in cell-free extracts in 1897 to be the birth of biochemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>  Some might also point as its beginning to the influential 1842 work by <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>, <i>Animal chemistry, or, Organic chemistry in its applications to physiology and pathology</i>, which presented a chemical theory of metabolism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_of_science_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-history_of_science-11">[11]</a></sup> or even earlier to the 18th century studies on <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Many other pioneers in the field who helped to uncover the layers of complexity of biochemistry have been proclaimed founders of modern biochemistry. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_Emil_Fischer" title="Hermann Emil Fischer">Emil Fischer</a>, who studied the chemistry of proteins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Gowland_Hopkins" title="Frederick Gowland Hopkins">F. Gowland Hopkins</a>, who studied enzymes and the dynamic nature of biochemistry, represent two examples of early biochemists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "biochemistry" itself  is derived from a combination of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>. In 1877, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Hoppe-Seyler" title="Felix Hoppe-Seyler">Felix Hoppe-Seyler</a> used the term (<i>biochemie</i> in German) as a synonym for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological_chemistry" title="Physiological chemistry">physiological chemistry</a> in the foreword to the first issue of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeitschrift_f%C3%BCr_Physiologische_Chemie" title="Zeitschrift für Physiologische Chemie">Zeitschrift für Physiologische Chemie</a></i> (Journal of Physiological Chemistry) where he argued for the setting up of institutes dedicated to this field of study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The German <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Neuberg" title="Carl Neuberg">Carl Neuberg</a> however is often cited to have coined the word in 1903,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben-Menahem_2009_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-Menahem_2009-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> while some credited it to <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hofmeister" title="Franz Hofmeister">Franz Hofmeister</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DNA_orbit_animated.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="290" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DNA_orbit_animated.gif/170px-DNA_orbit_animated.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DNA_orbit_animated.gif/255px-DNA_orbit_animated.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/DNA_orbit_animated.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DNA_orbit_animated.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>DNA structure (<span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.rcsb.org/structure/1D65" rel="nofollow">1D65</a></span>​)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It was once generally believed that life and its materials had some essential property or substance (often referred to as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vital_principle" title="Vital principle">vital principle</a>") distinct from any found in non-living matter, and it was thought that only living beings could produce the molecules of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1828, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> published a paper on his serendipitous <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> <a href="/wiki/W%C3%B6hler_synthesis" title="Wöhler synthesis">synthesis</a> from potassium cyanate and ammonium sulfate; some regarded that as a direct overthrow of vitalism and the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauffman_20012_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauffman_20012-29">[29]</a></sup>  However, the Wöhler synthesis has sparked controversy as some reject the death of vitalism at his hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>  Since then, biochemistry has advanced, especially since the mid-20th century, with the development of new techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual_polarisation_interferometry" title="Dual polarisation interferometry">dual polarisation interferometry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Protein nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotopic_labeling" title="Radioisotopic labeling">radioisotopic labeling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscopy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a> simulations. These techniques allowed for the discovery and detailed analysis of many molecules and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krebs_cycle" title="Krebs cycle">Krebs cycle</a> (citric acid cycle), and led to an understanding of biochemistry on a molecular level.
</p><p>Another significant historic event in biochemistry is the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a>, and its role in the transfer of information in the cell. In the 1950s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson">James D. Watson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a> were instrumental in solving DNA structure and suggesting its relationship with the genetic transfer of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1958, <a href="/wiki/George_Beadle" title="George Beadle">George Beadle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tatum" title="Edward Tatum">Edward Tatum</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> for work in fungi showing that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_gene-one_enzyme_hypothesis" title="One gene-one enzyme hypothesis">one gene produces one enzyme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krebs_2012_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs_2012-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1988, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Pitchfork" title="Colin Pitchfork">Colin Pitchfork</a> was the first person convicted of murder with <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> evidence, which led to the growth of <a href="/wiki/Forensic_science" title="Forensic science">forensic science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_2009_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butler_2009-33">[33]</a></sup> More recently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_Z._Fire" title="Andrew Z. Fire">Andrew Z. Fire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craig_C._Mello" title="Craig C. Mello">Craig C. Mello</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">2006 Nobel Prize</a> for discovering the role of <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> (RNAi), in the silencing of <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_2007-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Starting_materials:_the_chemical_elements_of_life">Starting materials: the chemical elements of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Starting materials: the chemical elements of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg/170px-201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg/255px-201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg/340px-201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:201_Elements_of_the_Human_Body.02.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main elements that compose the human body shown from most abundant (by mass) to least abundant.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">Composition of the human body</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">Dietary mineral</a></div>
<p>Around two dozen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_elements" title="Chemical elements">chemical elements</a> are essential to various kinds of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">biological life</a>. Most rare elements on Earth are not needed by life (exceptions being  <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> while a few common ones (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>) are not used. Most organisms share element needs, but there are a few differences between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">animals</a>. For example, ocean algae use <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>, but land plants and animals do not seem to need any. All animals require <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, but some plants do not. Plants need <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, but animals may not (or may need ultra-small amounts).
</p><p>Just six elements—<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>—make up almost 99% of the mass of living cells, including those in the human body (see <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">composition of the human body</a> for a complete list). In addition to the six major elements that compose most of the human body, humans require smaller amounts of possibly 18 more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomolecules">Biomolecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biomolecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">Biomolecule</a></div>
<p>The 4 main classes of molecules in bio-chemistry (often called <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a>) are <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slabaugh_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-slabaugh-37">[37]</a></sup> Many biological molecules are <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>: in this terminology, <a href="/wiki/Monomer" title="Monomer">monomers</a> are relatively small macromolecules that are linked together to create large <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> known as polymers. When monomers are linked together to synthesize a <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">biological polymer</a>, they undergo a process called <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration synthesis</a>. Different macromolecules can assemble in larger complexes, often needed for <a href="/wiki/Biological_activity" title="Biological activity">biological activity</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">Monosaccharide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">Disaccharide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">Polysaccharide</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</a></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta-D-Glucose.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="620" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharide</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sucrose-inkscape.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="434" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sucrose-inkscape.svg/220px-Sucrose-inkscape.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sucrose-inkscape.svg/330px-Sucrose-inkscape.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Sucrose-inkscape.svg/440px-Sucrose-inkscape.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A molecule of <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> + <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharide</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amylose_3Dprojection.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Amylose_3Dprojection.svg/220px-Amylose_3Dprojection.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Amylose_3Dprojection.svg/330px-Amylose_3Dprojection.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Amylose_3Dprojection.svg/440px-Amylose_3Dprojection.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Amylose" title="Amylose">Amylose</a>,  a <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> made up of several thousand <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> units</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Two of the main functions of carbohydrates are energy storage and providing structure. One of the common <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> known as glucose is a carbohydrate, but not all carbohydrates are sugars. There are more carbohydrates on Earth than any other known type of biomolecule; they are used to store energy and <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">genetic information</a>, as well as play important roles in cell to <a href="/wiki/Cell%E2%80%93cell_interaction" title="Cell–cell interaction">cell interactions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">communications</a>.
</p><p>The simplest type of carbohydrate is a <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharide</a>, which among other properties contains carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen, mostly in a ratio of 1:2:1 (generalized formula C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i></sub>O<sub><i>n</i></sub>, where <i>n</i> is at least 3). <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a>  (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) is one of the most important carbohydrates; others include <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>), the sugar commonly associated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_taste" title="Sweet taste">sweet taste</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whiting1970_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whiting1970-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_anone"><a href="#endnote_anone">[a]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), a component of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>.  A monosaccharide can switch between <a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic (open-chain) form</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_compound" title="Cyclic compound">cyclic</a> form.  The open-chain form can be turned into a ring of carbon atoms bridged by an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atom created from the <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_group" title="Carbonyl group">carbonyl group</a> of one end and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group of another. The cyclic molecule has a <a href="/wiki/Hemiacetal" title="Hemiacetal">hemiacetal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemiketal" title="Hemiketal">hemiketal</a> group, depending on whether the linear form was an <a href="/wiki/Aldose" title="Aldose">aldose</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Ketose" title="Ketose">ketose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In these cyclic forms, the ring usually has <b>5</b> or <b>6</b> atoms.  These forms are called <a href="/wiki/Furanose" title="Furanose">furanoses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyranose" title="Pyranose">pyranoses</a>, respectively—by analogy with <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">furan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyran" title="Pyran">pyran</a>, the simplest compounds with the same carbon-oxygen ring (although they lack the carbon-carbon <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonds</a> of these two molecules).  For example, the aldohexose <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> may form a hemiacetal linkage between the hydroxyl on carbon 1 and the oxygen on carbon 4, yielding a molecule with a 5-membered ring, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucofuranose" title="Glucofuranose">glucofuranose</a>.  The same reaction can take place between carbons 1 and 5 to form a molecule with a 6-membered ring, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucopyranose" title="Glucopyranose">glucopyranose</a>. Cyclic forms with a 7-atom ring called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heptoses" title="Heptoses">heptoses</a> are rare.
</p><p>Two monosaccharides can be joined by a <a href="/wiki/Glycosidic_bond" title="Glycosidic bond">glycosidic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ester_bond" title="Ester bond">ester bond</a> into a <i><a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharide</a></i> through a <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration reaction</a> during which a molecule of water is released. The reverse reaction in which the glycosidic bond of a disaccharide is broken into two monosaccharides is termed <i><a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a></i>.  The best-known disaccharide is <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> or ordinary <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, which consists of a <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> molecule and a <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> molecule joined. Another important disaccharide is <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> found in milk, consisting of a glucose molecule and a <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a> molecule. Lactose may be hydrolysed by <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a>, and deficiency in this enzyme results in <a href="/wiki/Lactose_intolerance" title="Lactose intolerance">lactose intolerance</a>.
</p><p>When a few (around three to six) monosaccharides are joined, it is called an <i><a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharide</a></i> (<i>oligo-</i> meaning "few"). These molecules tend to be used as markers and <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signals</a>, as well as having some other uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varki_1999_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varki_1999-40">[40]</a></sup> Many monosaccharides joined form a <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a>. They can be joined in one long linear chain, or they may be <a href="/wiki/Branching_(polymer_chemistry)" title="Branching (polymer chemistry)">branched</a>. Two of the most common polysaccharides are <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, both consisting of repeating glucose <a href="/wiki/Monomer" title="Monomer">monomers</a>.   <i>Cellulose</i> is an important structural component of plant's <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a></i> is used as a form of energy storage in animals.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">Sugar</a> can be characterized by having <a href="/wiki/Reducing_sugar" title="Reducing sugar">reducing</a> or non-reducing ends. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reducing_end" title="Reducing end">reducing end</a> of a carbohydrate is a carbon atom that can be in equilibrium with the open-chain <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehyde</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aldose" title="Aldose">aldose</a>) or keto form (<a href="/wiki/Ketose" title="Ketose">ketose</a>). If the joining of monomers takes place at such a carbon atom, the free hydroxy group of the <a href="/wiki/Pyranose" title="Pyranose">pyranose</a> or <a href="/wiki/Furanose" title="Furanose">furanose</a> form is exchanged with an OH-side-chain of another sugar, yielding a full <a href="/wiki/Acetal" title="Acetal">acetal</a>. This prevents opening of the chain to the aldehyde or keto form and renders the modified residue non-reducing. Lactose contains a reducing end at its glucose moiety, whereas the galactose moiety forms a full acetal with the C4-OH group of glucose. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccharose" title="Saccharose">Saccharose</a> does not have a reducing end because of full acetal formation between the aldehyde carbon of glucose (C1) and the keto carbon of fructose (C2).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lipids">Lipids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Lipids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">Lipid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">Glycerol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">Fatty acid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_lipids_lmaps.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1749" data-file-width="1755" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Common_lipids_lmaps.png/320px-Common_lipids_lmaps.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Common_lipids_lmaps.png/480px-Common_lipids_lmaps.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Common_lipids_lmaps.png/640px-Common_lipids_lmaps.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_lipids_lmaps.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structures of some common lipids. At the top are <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The middle structure is a <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglyceride</a> composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oleate" title="Oleate">oleoyl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stearic_acid" title="Stearic acid">stearoyl</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palmitate" title="Palmitate">palmitoyl</a> chains attached to a <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> backbone. At the bottom is the common <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphatidylcholine" title="Phosphatidylcholine">phosphatidylcholine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid"><b>Lipids</b></a> comprise a diverse range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a> and to some extent is a catchall for relatively water-insoluble or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonpolar" title="Nonpolar">nonpolar</a> compounds of biological origin, including <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, fatty-acid derived <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sphingolipid" title="Sphingolipid">sphingolipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycolipid" title="Glycolipid">glycolipids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">terpenoids</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Retinoid" title="Retinoid">retinoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">steroids</a>). Some lipids are linear, open-chain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliphatic" title="Aliphatic">aliphatic</a> molecules, while others have ring structures. Some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a> (with a cyclic [ring] and planar [flat] structure) while others are not. Some are flexible, while others are rigid.
</p><p>Lipids are usually made from one molecule of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> combined with other molecules. In <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglycerides</a>, the main group of bulk lipids, there is one molecule of glycerol and three <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>. Fatty acids are considered the monomer in that case, and may be <a href="/wiki/Saturated_and_unsaturated_compounds" title="Saturated and unsaturated compounds">saturated</a> (no <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonds</a> in the carbon chain) or unsaturated (one or more double bonds in the carbon chain).
</p><p>Most lipids have some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar</a> character in addition to being largely nonpolar. In general, the bulk of their structure is nonpolar or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> ("water-fearing"), meaning that it does not interact well with polar solvents like water. Another part of their structure is polar or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> ("water-loving") and will tend to associate with polar solvents like water. This makes them <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphiphilic" title="Amphiphilic">amphiphilic</a> molecules (having both hydrophobic and hydrophilic portions). In the case of <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a>, the polar group is a mere –OH (hydroxyl or alcohol). In the case of phospholipids, the polar groups are considerably larger and more polar, as described below.
</p><p>Lipids are an integral part of our daily diet. Most <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oils</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_product" title="Milk product">milk products</a> that we use for cooking and eating like <a href="/wiki/Butter" title="Butter">butter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghee" title="Ghee">ghee</a> etc., are composed of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">Vegetable oils</a> are rich in various <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acid" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acid">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a> (PUFA). Lipid-containing foods undergo digestion within the body and are broken into fatty acids and glycerol, which are the final degradation products of fats and lipids. Lipids, especially <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, are also used in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_product" title="Pharmaceutical product">pharmaceutical products</a>, either as co-solubilisers (e.g., in parenteral infusions) or else as <a href="/wiki/Drug_carrier" title="Drug carrier">drug carrier</a> components (e.g., in a <a href="/wiki/Liposome" title="Liposome">liposome</a> or <a href="/wiki/Transfersome" title="Transfersome">transfersome</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteins">Proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">Protein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AminoAcidball.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="702" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/160px-AminoAcidball.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/240px-AminoAcidball.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/AminoAcidball.svg/320px-AminoAcidball.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AminoAcidball.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The general structure of an α-amino acid, with the <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amino</a> group on the left and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboxyl" title="Carboxyl">carboxyl</a> group on the right.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">Proteins</a> are very large molecules—macro-biopolymers—made from monomers called <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>. An amino acid consists of an alpha carbon atom attached to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino" title="Amino">amino</a> group, –NH<sub>2</sub>, a <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a> group, –COOH (although these exist as –NH<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> and –COO<sup>−</sup> under physiologic conditions), a simple hydrogen atom, and a side chain commonly denoted as "–R". The side chain  "R" is different for each amino acid of which there are 20 <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">standard ones</a>.  It is this "R" group that made each amino acid different, and the properties of the side-chains greatly influence the overall <a href="/wiki/Protein_tertiary_structure" title="Protein tertiary structure">three-dimensional conformation</a> of a protein. Some amino acids have functions by themselves or in a modified form; for instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> functions as an important <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a>. Amino acids can be joined via a <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bond</a>. In this <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration</a> synthesis, a water molecule is removed and the peptide bond connects the nitrogen of one amino acid's amino group to the carbon of the other's carboxylic acid group. The resulting molecule is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Dipeptide" title="Dipeptide">dipeptide</a></i>, and short stretches of amino acids (usually, fewer than thirty) are called <i>peptides</i> or polypeptides. Longer stretches merit the title <i>proteins</i>. As an example, the important blood <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">serum</a> protein <a href="/wiki/Human_serum_albumin" title="Human serum albumin">albumin</a> contains 585 amino acid residues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzler_2001_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzler_2001-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amino_acids_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Amino_acids_1.png/390px-Amino_acids_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Amino_acids_1.png/585px-Amino_acids_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Amino_acids_1.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amino_acids_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Generic amino acids (1) in neutral form, (2) as they exist physiologically, and (3) joined as a dipeptide.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/160px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/240px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/320px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A schematic of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. The red and blue ribbons represent the protein <a href="/wiki/Globin" title="Globin">globin</a>; the green structures are the <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> groups.</div></div></div>
<p>Proteins can have structural and/or functional roles. For instance, movements of the proteins <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> ultimately are responsible for the contraction of skeletal muscle. One property many proteins have is that they specifically bind to a certain molecule or class of molecules—they may be <i>extremely</i> selective in what they bind. <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">Antibodies</a> are an example of proteins that attach to one specific type of molecule. Antibodies are composed of heavy and light chains. Two heavy chains would be linked to two light chains through disulfide linkages between their amino acids. Antibodies are specific through variation based on differences in the N-terminal domain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzyme-linked_immunosorbent_assay" title="Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay">enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay</a> (ELISA), which uses antibodies, is one of the most sensitive tests modern medicine uses to detect various biomolecules. Probably the most important proteins, however, are the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. Virtually every reaction in a living cell requires an enzyme to lower the activation energy of the reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup> These molecules recognize specific reactant molecules called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrates</a></i>; they then <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyze</a> the reaction between them. By lowering the <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a>, the enzyme speeds up that reaction by a rate of 10<sup>11</sup> or more;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup> a reaction that would normally take over 3,000 years to complete spontaneously might take less than a second with an enzyme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The enzyme itself is not used up in the process and is free to catalyze the same reaction with a new set of substrates. Using various modifiers, the activity of the enzyme can be regulated, enabling control of the biochemistry of the cell as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The structure of proteins is traditionally described in a hierarchy of four levels. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_structure" title="Primary structure">primary structure</a> of a protein consists of its linear sequence of amino acids; for instance, "alanine-glycine-tryptophan-serine-glutamate-asparagine-glycine-lysine-…". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">Secondary structure</a> is concerned with local morphology (morphology being the study of structure). Some combinations of amino acids will tend to curl up in a coil called an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_helix" title="Alpha helix">α-helix</a> or into a sheet called a <a href="/wiki/Beta_sheet" title="Beta sheet">β-sheet</a>; some α-helixes can be seen in the hemoglobin schematic above. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_structure" title="Tertiary structure">Tertiary structure</a> is the entire three-dimensional shape of the protein. This shape is determined by the sequence of amino acids. In fact, a single change can change the entire structure. The alpha chain of hemoglobin contains 146 amino acid residues; substitution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> residue at position 6 with a <a href="/wiki/Valine" title="Valine">valine</a> residue changes the behavior of hemoglobin so much that it results in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_disease" title="Sickle-cell disease">sickle-cell disease</a>. Finally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaternary_structure" title="Quaternary structure">quaternary structure</a> is concerned with the structure of a protein with multiple peptide subunits, like hemoglobin with its four subunits. Not all proteins have more than one subunit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protein_structure_examples.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1938" data-file-width="1571" decoding="async" height="987" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Protein_structure_examples.png/800px-Protein_structure_examples.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Protein_structure_examples.png/1200px-Protein_structure_examples.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Protein_structure_examples.png 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Protein_structure_examples.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Examples of protein structures from the <a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">Protein Data Bank</a></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3677" data-file-width="2675" decoding="async" height="481" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png/350px-Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png/525px-Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png/700px-Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Structural_coverage_of_the_human_cyclophilin_family.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of a protein family, as represented by the structures of the <a href="/wiki/Isomerase" title="Isomerase">isomerase</a> <a href="/wiki/Protein_domain" title="Protein domain">domains</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ingested proteins are usually broken up into single amino acids or dipeptides in the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a> and then absorbed. They can then be joined to form new proteins. Intermediate products of glycolysis, the citric acid cycle, and the <a href="/wiki/Pentose_phosphate_pathway" title="Pentose phosphate pathway">pentose phosphate pathway</a> can be used to form all twenty amino acids, and most bacteria and plants possess all the necessary enzymes to synthesize them. Humans and other mammals, however, can synthesize only half of them. They cannot synthesize <a href="/wiki/Isoleucine" title="Isoleucine">isoleucine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">leucine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">phenylalanine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">threonine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">tryptophan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Valine" title="Valine">valine</a>. Because they must be ingested, these are the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>. Mammals do possess the enzymes to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Alanine" title="Alanine">alanine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asparagine" title="Asparagine">asparagine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serine" title="Serine">serine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a>, the nonessential amino acids. While they can synthesize <a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine">arginine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">histidine</a>, they cannot produce it in sufficient amounts for young, growing animals, and so these are often considered essential amino acids.
</p><p>If the amino group is removed from an amino acid, it leaves behind a carbon skeleton called an α-<a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">keto acid</a>. Enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminases</a> can easily transfer the amino group from one amino acid (making it an α-keto acid) to another α-keto acid (making it an amino acid). This is important in the biosynthesis of amino acids, as for many of the pathways, intermediates from other biochemical pathways are converted to the α-keto acid skeleton, and then an amino group is added, often via <a href="/wiki/Transamination" title="Transamination">transamination</a>. The amino acids may then be linked together to form a protein.
</p><p>A similar process is used to break down proteins. It is first hydrolyzed into its component amino acids. Free <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH3), existing as the <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> ion (NH4+) in blood, is toxic to life forms. A suitable method for excreting it must therefore exist. Different tactics have evolved in different animals, depending on the animals' needs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">Unicellular</a> organisms simply release the ammonia into the environment. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">bony fish</a> can release the ammonia into the water where it is quickly diluted. In general, mammals convert the ammonia into urea, via the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>.
</p><p>In order to determine whether two proteins are related, or in other words to decide whether they are homologous or not, scientists use sequence-comparison methods. Methods like <a href="/wiki/Sequence_alignment" title="Sequence alignment">sequence alignments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Structural_alignment" title="Structural alignment">structural alignments</a> are powerful tools that help scientists identify <a href="/wiki/Sequence_homology" title="Sequence homology">homologies</a> between related molecules. The relevance of finding homologies among proteins goes beyond forming an evolutionary pattern of <a href="/wiki/Protein_family" title="Protein family">protein families</a>. By finding how similar two protein sequences are, we acquire knowledge about their structure and therefore their function.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleic_acids">Nucleic acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nucleic acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">Nucleic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">Nucleotide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="822" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg/350px-0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg/525px-0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg/700px-0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0322_DNA_Nucleotides.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA), the picture shows the monomers being put together.</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acids" title="Nucleic acids">Nucleic acids</a>, so-called because of their prevalence in cellular <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a>, is the generic name of the family of biopolymers. They are complex, high-molecular-weight biochemical macromolecules that can convey <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_information" title="Genetic information">genetic information</a> in all living cells and viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voet_2005_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_2005-2">[2]</a></sup> The monomers are called <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, and each consists of three components: a nitrogenous heterocyclic <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> (either a <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purine</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a>), a pentose sugar, and a <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nucleotides_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="820" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nucleotides_1.svg/500px-Nucleotides_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nucleotides_1.svg/750px-Nucleotides_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nucleotides_1.svg/1000px-Nucleotides_1.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nucleotides_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structural elements of common nucleic acid constituents. Because they contain at least one phosphate group, the compounds marked <i>nucleoside monophosphate</i>, <i>nucleoside diphosphate</i> and <i>nucleoside triphosphate</i> are all nucleotides (not simply phosphate-lacking <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleosides</a>).</div></div></div></div>
<p>The most common nucleic acids are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribonucleic_acid" title="Ribonucleic acid">ribonucleic acid</a> (RNA). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphate_group" title="Phosphate group">phosphate group</a> and the sugar of each nucleotide bond with each other to form the backbone of the nucleic acid, while the sequence of nitrogenous bases stores the information. The most common nitrogenous bases are <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_base" title="Nitrogenous base">nitrogenous bases</a> of each strand of a nucleic acid will form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonds" title="Hydrogen bonds">hydrogen bonds</a> with certain other nitrogenous bases in a complementary strand of nucleic acid (similar to a zipper). Adenine binds with thymine and uracil, thymine binds only with adenine, and cytosine and guanine can bind only with one another. Adenine and Thymine &amp; Adenine and Uracil contains two hydrogen Bonds, while Hydrogen Bonds formed between cytosine and guanine are three in number.
</p><p>Aside from the genetic material of the cell, nucleic acids often play a role as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_messenger" title="Second messenger">second messengers</a>, as well as forming the base molecule for <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the primary energy-carrier molecule found in all living organisms. Also, the nitrogenous bases possible in the two nucleic acids are different: adenine, cytosine, and guanine occur in both RNA and DNA, while thymine occurs only in DNA and uracil occurs in RNA.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates_as_energy_source">Carbohydrates as energy source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates as energy source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate_metabolism" title="Carbohydrate metabolism">Carbohydrate metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<p>Glucose is an energy source in most life forms. For instance, polysaccharides are broken down into their monomers by <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glycogen_phosphorylase" title="Glycogen phosphorylase">glycogen phosphorylase</a> removes glucose residues from glycogen, a polysaccharide). Disaccharides like lactose or sucrose are cleaved into their two component monosaccharides.
</p>
<h4><span id="Glycolysis_.28anaerobic.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Glycolysis_(anaerobic)">Glycolysis (anaerobic)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Glycolysis (anaerobic)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
