</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><div style="float:left;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="133" decoding="async" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18"/></div>The <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathway</a> of glycolysis converts <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a> via a series of intermediate metabolites. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:pink; color:black;"> </span> </span> Each chemical modification is performed by a different enzyme. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#8f8fda; color:black;"> </span> </span> Steps 1 and 3 consume <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e0d10f; color:black;"> </span> </span> steps 7 and 10 produce ATP. Since steps 6–10 occur twice per glucose molecule, this leads to a net production of ATP.</div>
</div>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Glucose is mainly metabolized by a very important ten-step <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">pathway</a> called <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a>, the net result of which is to break down one molecule of glucose into two molecules of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>. This also produces a net two molecules of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>, the energy currency of cells, along with two reducing equivalents of converting <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">NAD<sup>+</sup></a> (nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide: oxidized form) to NADH (nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide: reduced form). This does not require oxygen; if no oxygen is available (or the cell cannot use oxygen), the NAD is restored by converting the pyruvate to <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate (lactic acid)</a> (e.g., in humans) or to <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> plus carbon dioxide (e.g., in <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>). Other monosaccharides like galactose and fructose can be converted into intermediates of the glycolytic pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerobic">Aerobic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Aerobic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_glycolysis" title="Aerobic glycolysis">aerobic</a> cells with sufficient oxygen, as in most human cells, the pyruvate is further metabolized. It is irreversibly converted to <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a>, giving off one carbon atom as the waste product <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, generating another reducing equivalent as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADH" title="NADH">NADH</a>. The two molecules acetyl-CoA (from one molecule of glucose) then enter the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>, producing two molecules of ATP, six more NADH molecules and two reduced (ubi)quinones (via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FADH2" title="FADH2">FADH<sub>2</sub></a> as enzyme-bound cofactor), and releasing the remaining carbon atoms as carbon dioxide. The produced NADH and quinol molecules then feed into the enzyme complexes of the respiratory chain, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_transport_system" title="Electron transport system">electron transport system</a> transferring the electrons ultimately to oxygen and conserving the released energy in the form of a proton gradient over a membrane (<a href="/wiki/Inner_mitochondrial_membrane" title="Inner mitochondrial membrane">inner mitochondrial membrane</a> in eukaryotes). Thus, oxygen is reduced to water and the original electron acceptors NAD<sup>+</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Quinone" title="Quinone">quinone</a> are regenerated. This is why humans breathe in oxygen and breathe out carbon dioxide. The energy released from transferring the electrons from high-energy states in NADH and quinol is conserved first as proton gradient and converted to ATP via ATP synthase. This generates an additional <i>28</i> molecules of ATP (24 from the 8 NADH + 4 from the 2 quinols), totaling to 32 molecules of ATP conserved per degraded glucose (two from glycolysis + two from the citrate cycle).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It is clear that using oxygen to completely oxidize glucose provides an organism with far more energy than any oxygen-independent metabolic feature, and this is thought to be the reason why complex life appeared only after Earth's atmosphere accumulated large amounts of oxygen.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gluconeogenesis">Gluconeogenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gluconeogenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">Gluconeogenesis</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, vigorously contracting <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscles</a> (during weightlifting or sprinting, for example) do not receive enough oxygen to meet the energy demand, and so they shift to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">anaerobic metabolism</a>, converting glucose to lactate. 
The combination of glucose from noncarbohydrates origin, such as fat and proteins. This only happens when <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> supplies in the liver are worn out. The pathway is a crucial reversal of <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> from pyruvate to glucose and can utilize many sources like amino acids, glycerol and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krebs_Cycle" title="Krebs Cycle">Krebs Cycle</a>. Large scale protein and fat <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a> usually occur when those suffer from starvation or certain endocrine disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> regenerates the glucose, using a process called <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>. This process is not quite the opposite of glycolysis, and actually requires three times the amount of energy gained from glycolysis (six molecules of ATP are used, compared to the two gained in glycolysis). Analogous to the above reactions, the glucose produced can then undergo glycolysis in tissues that need energy, be stored as glycogen (or <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> in plants), or be converted to other monosaccharides or joined into di- or oligosaccharides. The combined pathways of glycolysis during exercise, lactate's crossing via the bloodstream to the liver, subsequent gluconeogenesis and release of glucose into the bloodstream is called the <a href="/wiki/Cori_cycle" title="Cori cycle">Cori cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Relationship_to_other_.22molecular-scale.22_biological_sciences"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Relationship_to_other_"molecular-scale"_biological_sciences'>Relationship to other "molecular-scale" biological sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title='Edit section: Relationship to other "molecular-scale" biological sciences'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry,_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="875" data-file-width="906" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/250px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/375px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg/500px-Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry%2C_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_relationship_between_biochemistry,_genetics_and_molecular_biology.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic relationship between biochemistry, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Researchers in biochemistry use specific techniques native to biochemistry, but increasingly combine these with techniques and ideas developed in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysics</a>. There is not a defined line between these disciplines. Biochemistry studies the <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> required for biological activity of molecules, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> studies their biological activity, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> studies their heredity, which happens to be carried by their <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>. This is shown in the following schematic that depicts one possible view of the relationships between the fields:
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>Biochemistry</b></i> is the study of the chemical substances and vital processes occurring in live <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biochemist" title="Biochemist">Biochemists</a> focus heavily on the role, function, and structure of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a>. The study of the chemistry behind biological processes and the synthesis of biologically active molecules are applications of biochemistry. Biochemistry studies life at the atomic and molecular level.</li>
<li><i><b>Genetics</b></i> is the study of the effect of genetic differences in organisms. This can often be inferred by the absence of a normal component (e.g. one <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a>). The study of "<a href="/wiki/Mutant" title="Mutant">mutants</a>" – organisms that lack one or more functional components with respect to the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Wild_type" title="Wild type">wild type</a>" or normal <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>. Genetic interactions (<a href="/wiki/Epistasis" title="Epistasis">epistasis</a>) can often confound simple interpretations of such "<a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">knockout</a>" studies.</li>
<li><i><b>Molecular biology</b></i> is the study of molecular underpinnings of the biological phenomena, focusing on molecular synthesis, modification, mechanisms and interactions. The <a href="/wiki/Central_dogma_of_molecular_biology" title="Central dogma of molecular biology">central dogma of molecular biology</a>, where genetic material is transcribed into RNA and then translated into <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, despite being oversimplified, still provides a good starting point for understanding the field. This concept has been revised in light of emerging novel roles for <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>.</li>
<li>'<b><a href="/wiki/Chemical_biology" title="Chemical biology">Chemical biology</a>'</b> seeks to develop new tools based on <a href="/wiki/Small_molecule" title="Small molecule">small molecules</a> that allow minimal perturbation of biological systems while providing detailed information about their function. Further, chemical biology employs biological systems to create non-natural hybrids between biomolecules and synthetic devices (for example emptied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viral_capsid" title="Viral capsid">viral capsids</a> that can deliver <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">drug molecules</a>).</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">
<p><b>a.</b> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_anone"><a href="#ref_anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Fructose is not the only sugar found in fruits. Glucose and sucrose are also found in varying quantities in various fruits, and sometimes exceed the fructose present. For example, 32% of the edible portion of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Date_(fruit)" title="Date (fruit)">date</a> is glucose, compared with 24% fructose and 8% sucrose. However, <a href="/wiki/Peach" title="Peach">peaches</a> contain more sucrose (6.66%) than they do fructose (0.93%) or glucose (1.47%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whiting1970p5_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whiting1970p5-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biochemistry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
